On Saturday the headline be `` Even Berlin Admits Offensive Checked , '' and for the first time in that terrible week the Ingleside folk dare to draw a long breath .
`` Well , we have get one week over -- now for the next , '' say Susan staunchly .
`` I feel like a prisoner on the rack when they stop turn it , '' Miss Oliver say to Rilla , a they go to church on Easter morning .
`` But I be not off the rack .
The torture may begin again at any time . ''
`` I doubt God last Sunday , '' say Rilla , `` but I do n't doubt him today .
Evil can not win .
Spirit be on our side and it be bind to outlast flesh . ''
Nevertheless her faith be often try in the dark spring that follow .
Armageddon be not , a they have hop , a matter of a few day .
It stretch out into week and month .
Again and again Hindenburg strike his savage , sudden blow , with alarming , though futile success .
Again and again the military critic declare the situation extremely perilous .
Again and again Cousin Sophia agree with the military critic .
`` If the Allies go back three mile more the war be lose , '' she wail .
`` Is the British navy anchor in those three mile ? ''
demand Susan scornfully .
`` It be the opinion of a man who know all about it , '' say Cousin Sophia solemnly .
`` There be no such person , '' retort Susan .
`` As for the military critic , they do not know one blessed thing about it , any more than you or I .
They have be mistake time out of number .
Why do you always look on the dark side , Sophia Crawford ? ''
`` Because there ai n't any bright side , Susan Baker . ''
`` Oh , be there not ?
It be the twentieth of April , and Hindy be not in Paris yet , although he say he would be there by April first .
Is that not a bright spot at least ? ''
`` It be my opinion that the Germans will be in Paris before very long and more than that , Susan Baker , they will be in Canada . ''
`` Not in this part of it .
The Huns shall never set foot in Prince Edward Island as long a I can handle a pitchfork , '' declare Susan , look , and feel quite equal to rout the entire German army single-handed .
`` No , Sophia Crawford , to tell you the plain truth I be sick and tired of your gloomy prediction .
I do not deny that some mistake have be make .
The Germans would never have get back Passchendaele if the Canadians have be leave there ; and it be bad business trust to those Portuguese at the Lys River .
But that be no reason why you or anyone should go about proclaim the war be lose .
I do not want to quarrel with you , least of all at such a time a this , but our morale must be keep up , and I be go to speak my mind out plainly and tell you that if you can not keep from such croak your room be good than your company . ''
Cousin Sophia march home in high dudgeon to digest her affront , and do not reappear in Susan 's kitchen for many week .
Perhaps it be just as well , for they be hard week , when the Germans continue to strike , now here , now there , and seemingly vital point fell to them at every blow .
And one day in early May , when wind and sunshine frolic in Rainbow Valley and the maple grove be golden-green and the harbour all blue and dimpled and white-capped , the news come about Jem .
There have be a trench raid on the Canadian front -- a little trench raid so insignificant that it be never even mention in the dispatch and when it be over Lieutenant James Blythe be report `` wounded and miss . ''
`` I think this be even bad than the news of his death would have be , '' moan Rilla through her white lip , that night .
`` No -- no -- ` miss ' leave a little hope , Rilla , '' urge Gertrude Oliver .
`` Yes -- torturing , agonize hope that keep you from ever become quite resign to the bad , '' say Rilla .
`` Oh , Miss Oliver -- must we go for week and month -- not know whether Jem be alive or dead ?
Perhaps we will never know .
I -- I can not bear it -- I can not .
Walter -- and now Jem .
This will kill mother -- look at her face , Miss Oliver , and you will see that .
And Faith -- poor Faith -- how can she bear it ? ''
Gertrude shiver with pain .
She look up at the picture hang over Rilla 's desk and felt a sudden hatred of Mona Lisa 's endless smile .
`` Will not even this blot it off your face ? ''
she think savagely .
But she say gently , `` No , it wo n't kill your mother .
She 's make of finer mettle than that .
Besides , she refuse to believe Jem be dead ; she will cling to hope and we must all do that .
Faith , you may be sure , will do it . ''
`` I can not , '' moan Rilla , `` Jem be wound -- what chance would he have ?
Even if the Germans find him -- we know how they have treat wounded prisoner .
I wish I could hope , Miss Oliver -- it would help , I suppose .
But hope seem dead in me .
I ca n't hope without some reason for it -- and there be no reason . ''
When Miss Oliver have go to her own room and Rilla be lie on her bed in the moonlight , pray desperately for a little strength , Susan step in like a gaunt shadow and sit down beside her .
`` Rilla , dear , do not you worry .
Little Jem be not dead . ''
`` Oh , how can you believe that , Susan ? ''
`` Because I know .
Listen you to me .
When that word come this morning the first thing I think of be Dog Monday .
And tonight , as soon a I get the supper dish wash and the bread set , I go down to the station .
There be Dog Monday , wait for the train , just as patient a usual .
Now , Rilla , dear , that trench raid be four day ago -- last Monday -- and I say to the station-agent , ` Can you tell me if that dog howl or make any kind of a fuss last Monday night ? '
He think it over a bit , and then he say , ` No , he do not . '
` Are you sure ? '
I say .
` There 's more depends on it than you think ! '
` Dead sure , ' he say . '
I be up all night last Monday night because my mare be sick , and there be never a sound out of him .
I would have hear if there have be , for the stable door be open all the time and his kennel be right across from it ! '
Now Rilla dear , those be the man 's very word .
And you know how that poor little dog howl all night after the battle of Courcelette .
Yet he do not love Walter as much a he love Jem .
If he mourn for Walter like that , do you suppose he would sleep sound in his kennel the night after Jem have be kill ?
No , Rilla dear , little Jem be not dead , and that you may tie to .
If he be , Dog Monday would have know , just a he know before , and he would not be still wait for the train . ''
It be absurd -- and irrational -- and impossible .
But Rilla believe it , for all that ; and Mrs. Blythe believe it ; and the doctor , though he smile faintly in pretended derision , felt an odd confidence replace his first despair ; and foolish and absurd or not , they all pluck up heart and courage to carry on , just because a faithful little dog at the Glen station be still watch with unbroken faith for his master to come home .
Common sense might scorn -- incredulity might mutter `` Mere superstition '' -- but in their heart the folk of Ingleside stood by their belief that Dog Monday know .
CHAPTER XXX THE TURNING OF THE TIDE Susan be very sorrowful when she saw the beautiful old lawn of Ingleside plough up that spring and plant with potato .
Yet she make no protest , even when her beloved peony bed be sacrifice .
But when the Government pass the Daylight Saving law Susan balk .
There be a Higher Power than the Union Government , to which Susan owe allegiance .
`` Do you think it right to meddle with the arrangement of the Almighty ? ''
she demand indignantly of the doctor .
The doctor , quite unmoved , respond that the law must be observe , and the Ingleside clock be move on accordingly .
But the doctor have no power over Susan 's little alarm .
`` I buy that with my own money , Mrs. Dr. dear , '' she say firmly , `` and it shall go on God 's time and not Borden 's time . ''
Susan get up and go to bed by `` God 's time , '' and regulate her own going and coming by it .
She serve the meal , under protest , by Borden 's time , and she have to go to church by it , which be the crowning injury .
But she say her prayer by her own clock , and feed the hen by it ; so that there be always a furtive triumph in her eye when she look at the doctor .
She have get the good of him by so much at least .
`` Whiskers-on-the-moon be very much delight with this daylight saving business , '' she tell him one evening .
`` Of course he naturally would be , since I understand that the Germans invent it .
I hear he come near lose his entire wheat-crop lately .
Warren Mead 's cow break into the field one day last week -- it be the very day the Germans capture the Chemang-de-dam , which may have be a coincidence or may not -- and be make fine havoc of it when Mrs. Dick Clow happen to see them from her attic window .
At first she have no intention of let Mr. Pryor know .
She tell me she have just gloat over the sight of those cow pasture on his wheat .
She felt it serve him exactly right .
But presently she reflect that the wheat-crop be a matter of great importance and that ` save and serve ' meant that those cow must be rout out as much a it mean anything .
So she go down and phone over to Whiskers about the matter .
All the thanks she get be that he say something queer right out to her .
She be not prepared to state that it be actually swear for you can not be sure just what you hear over the phone ; but she have her own opinion , and so have I , but I will not express it for here come Mr. Meredith , and Whiskers be one of his elder , so we must be discreet . ''
`` Are you look for the new star ? ''
ask Mr. Meredith , join Miss Oliver and Rilla , who be stand among the blossoming potato gaze skyward .
`` Yes -- we have find it -- see , it be just above the tip of the tall old pine . ''
`` It 's wonderful to be look at something that happen three thousand year ago , be n't it ? ''
say Rilla .
`` That be when astronomer think the collision take place which produce this new star .
It make me feel horribly insignificant , '' she add under her breath .
`` Even this event can not dwarf into what may be the proper perspective in star system the fact that the Germans be again only one leap from Paris , '' say Gertrude restlessly .
`` I think I would like to have be an astronomer , '' say Mr. Meredith dreamily , gaze at the star .
`` There must be a strange pleasure in it , '' agree Miss Oliver , `` an unearthly pleasure , in more sens than one .
I would like to have a few astronomer for my friend . ''
`` Fancy talk the gossip of the host of heaven , '' laugh Rilla .
`` I wonder if astronomer feel a very deep interest in earthly affair ? ''
say the doctor .
`` Perhaps student of the canal of Mars would not be so keenly sensitive to the significance of a few yard of trench lose or win on the western front . ''
`` I have read somewhere , '' say Mr. Meredith , `` that Ernest Renan write one of his book during the siege of Paris in 1870 and ` enjoy the writing of it very much . '
I suppose one would call him a philosopher . ''
`` I have read also , '' say Miss Oliver , `` that shortly before his death he say that his only regret in die be that he must die before he have see what that ` extremely interesting young man , the German Emperor , ' would do in his life .
If Ernest Renan ` walk ' today and saw what that interest young man have do to his beloved France , not to speak of the world , I wonder if his mental detachment would be as complete a it be in 1870 . ''
`` I wonder where Jem be tonight , '' think Rilla , in a sudden bitter inrush of remembrance .
It be over a month since the news have come about Jem .
Nothing have be discover concern him , in spite of all effort .
Two or three letter have come from him , write before the trench raid , and since then there have be only unbroken silence .
Now the Germans be again at the Marne , press nearer and near Paris ; now rumour be come of another Austrian offensive against the Piave line .
Rilla turn away from the new star , sick at heart .
It be one of the moment when hope and courage fail her utterly -- when it seem impossible to go on even one more day .
If only they know what have happen to Jem -- you can face anything you know .
But a beleaguerment of fear and doubt and suspense be a hard thing for the morale .
Surely , if Jem be alive , some word would have come through .
He must be dead .
Only -- they would never know -- they could never be quite sure ; and Dog Monday would wait for the train until he die of old age .
Monday be only a poor , faithful , rheumatic little dog , who know nothing more of his master 's fate than they do .
Rilla have a `` white night '' and do not fall asleep until late .
When she waken Gertrude Oliver be sit at her window lean out to meet the silver mystery of the dawn .
Her clever , strike profile , with the mass of black hair behind it , come out clearly against the pallid gold of the eastern sky .
Rilla remember Jem 's admiration of the curve of Miss Oliver 's brow and chin , and she shudder .
Everything that remind her of Jem be begin to give intolerable pain .
Walter 's death have inflict on her heart a terrible wound .
But it have be a clean wound and have heal slowly , a such wound do , though the scar must remain for ever .
But the torture of Jem 's disappearance be another thing : there be a poison in it that keep it from heal .
The alternation of hope and despair , the endless watch each day for the letter that never come -- that might never come -- the newspaper tale of ill-usage of prisoner -- the bitter wonder a to Jem 's wound -- all be increasingly hard to bear .
Gertrude Oliver turn her head .
There be an odd brilliancy in her eye .
`` Rilla , I 've have another dream . ''
`` Oh , no -- no , '' cry Rilla , shrink .
Miss Oliver 's dream have always foretell coming disaster .
`` Rilla , it be a good dream .
Listen -- I dream just a I do four year ago , that I stand on the veranda step and look down the Glen .
And it be still cover by wave that lap about my foot .
But a I look the wave begin to ebb -- and they ebb as swiftly a , four year ago , they roll in -- ebb out and out , to the gulf ; and the Glen lay before me , beautiful and green , with a rainbow span Rainbow Valley -- a rainbow of such splendid colour that it dazzle me -- and I wake .
Rilla -- Rilla Blythe -- the tide have turn . ''
`` I wish I could believe it , '' sigh Rilla .
`` Sooth be my prophecy of fear Believe it when it augur cheer , '' quote Gertrude , almost gaily .
`` I tell you I have no doubt . ''
Yet , in spite of the great Italian victory at the Piave that come a few day later , she have doubt many a time in the hard month that follow ; and when in mid-July the Germans cross the Marne again despair come sickeningly .
It be idle , they all felt , to hope that the miracle of the Marne would be repeat .
But it be : again , a in 1914 , the tide turn at the Marne .
The French and the American troop strike their sudden smashing blow on the exposed flank of the enemy and , with the almost inconceivable rapidity of a dream , the whole aspect of the war change .
`` The Allies have win two tremendous victory , '' say the doctor on 20th July .
`` It be the beginning of the end -- I feel it -- I feel it , '' say Mrs. Blythe .
`` Thank God , '' say Susan , fold her tremble old hand , Then she add , under her breath , `` but it wo n't bring our boy back . ''
Nevertheless she go out and run up the flag , for the first time since the fall of Jerusalem .
As it catch the breeze and swell gallantly out above her , Susan lift her hand and salute it , a she have see Shirley do .
`` We 've all give something to keep you fly , '' she say .
`` Four hundred thousand of our boy go overseas -- fifty thousand of them kill .
But -- you be worth it ! ''
The wind whip her grey hair about her face and the gingham apron that shroud her from head to foot be cut on line of economy , not of grace ; yet , somehow , just then Susan make an imposing figure .
She be one of the woman -- courageous , unquailing , patient , heroic -- who have make victory possible .
In her , they all salute the symbol for which their dearest have fight .
Something of this be in the doctor 's mind a he watch her from the door .
`` Susan , '' he say , when she turn to come in , `` from first to last of this business you have be a brick ! ''
CHAPTER XXXI MRS. MATILDA PITTMAN Rilla and Jims be stand on the rear platform of their car when the train stop at the little Millward siding .
The August evening be so hot and close that the crowded car be stifle .
Nobody ever know just why train stop at Millward side .
Nobody be ever know to get off there or get on .
There be only one house nearer to it than four mile , and it be surround by acre of blueberry barren and scrub spruce-trees .
Rilla be on her way into Charlottetown to spend the night with a friend and the next day in Red Cross shopping ; she have take Jims with her , partly because she do not want Susan or her mother to be bother with his care , partly because of a hungry desire in her heart to have as much of him a she could before she might have to give him up forever .
James Anderson have write to her not long before this ; he be wound and in the hospital ; he would not be able to go back to the front and as soon a he be able he would be come home for Jims .
Rilla be heavy-hearted over this , and worry also .
She love Jims dearly and would feel deeply give him up in any case ; but if Jim Anderson be a different sort of a man , with a proper home for the child , it would not be so bad .
But to give Jims up to a roving , shiftless , irresponsible father , however kind and good-hearted he might be -- and she know Jim Anderson be kind and good-hearted enough -- be a bitter prospect to Rilla .
It be not even likely Anderson would stay in the Glen ; he have no tie there now ; he might even go back to England .
She might never see her dear , sunshiny , carefully brought-up little Jims again .
With such a father what might his fate be ?
Rilla meant to beg Jim Anderson to leave him with her , but , from his letter , she have not much hope that he would .
`` If he would only stay in the Glen , where I could keep an eye on Jims and have him often with me I would n't feel so worried over it , '' she reflect .
`` But I feel sure he wo n't -- and Jims will never have any chance .
And he be such a bright little chap -- he have ambition , wherever he get it -- and he be n't lazy .
But his father will never have a cent to give him any education or start in life .
Jims , my little war-baby , whatever be go to become of you ? ''
Jims be not in the least concerned over what be to become of him .
He be gleefully watch the antic of a striped chipmunk that be frisk over the roof of the little siding .
As the train pull out Jims lean eagerly forward for a last look at Chippy , pull his hand from Rilla 's .
Rilla be so engrossed in wonder what be to become of Jims in the future that she forget to take notice of what be happen to him in the present .
What do happen be that Jims lose his balance , shot headlong down the step , hurtle across the little siding platform , and land in a clump of bracken fern on the other side .
Rilla shriek and lose her head .
She spring down the step and jump off the train .
Fortunately , the train be still go at a comparatively slow speed ; fortunately also , Rilla retain enough sense to jump the way it be go ; nevertheless , she fell and sprawl helplessly down the embankment , land in a ditch full of a rank growth of golden-rod and fireweed .
Nobody have see what have happen and the train whisk briskly away round a curve in the barren .
Rilla pick herself up , dizzy but unhurt , scramble out of the ditch , and fly wildly across the platform , expect to find Jims dead or break in piece .
But Jims , except for a few bruise , and a big fright , be quite uninjured .
He be so badly scar that he do n't even cry , but Rilla , when she find that he be safe and sound , burst into tear and sob wildly .
`` Nasty old twain , '' remark Jims in disgust .
`` And nasty old God , '' he add , with a scowl at the heaven .
A laugh broke into Rilla 's sobbing , produce something very like what her father would have call hysteric .
But she catch herself up before the hysteria could conquer her .
`` Rilla Blythe , I 'm ashamed of you .
Pull yourself together immediately .
Jims , you should n't have say anything like that . ''
`` God frew me off the twain , '' declare Jims defiantly .
`` Somebody frew me ; you do n't frow me ; so it be God . ''
`` No , it be n't .
You fell because you let go of my hand and bent too far forward .
I tell you not to do that .
So that it be your own fault . ''
Jims look to see if she mean it ; then glance up at the sky again .
`` Excuse me , then , God , '' he remark airily .
Rilla scan the sky also ; she do not like it appearance ; a heavy thundercloud be appear in the northwest .
What in the world be to be do ?
There be no other train that night , since the nine o'clock special run only on Saturdays .
Would it be possible for them to reach Hannah Brewster 's house , two mile away , before the storm break ?
Rilla think she could do it alone easily enough , but with Jims it be another matter .
Were his little legs good for it ?
`` We 've get to try it , '' say Rilla desperately .
`` We might stay in the siding until the thunderstorm be over ; but it may keep on rain all night and anyway it will be pitch dark .
If we can get to Hannah 's she will keep u all night . ''
Hannah Brewster , when she have be Hannah Crawford , have live in the Glen and go to school with Rilla .
They have be good friend then , though Hannah have be three year the old .
She have marry very young and have go to live in Millward .
What with hard work and baby and a ne'er - do-well husband , her life have not be an easy one , and Hannah seldom revisit her old home .
Rilla have visit her once soon after her marriage , but have not see her or even heard of her for year ; she know , however , that she and Jims would find welcome and harbourage in any house where rosy-faced , open-hearted , generous Hannah live .

The road , seldom use , be rough and deep-rutted .
Jims grow so tired that Rilla have to carry him for the last quarter .
She reach the Brewster house , almost exhaust , and drop Jims on the walk with a sigh of thankfulness .
The sky be black with cloud ; the first heavy drop be begin to fall ; and the rumble of thunder be grow very loud .
Then she make an unpleasant discovery .
The blind be all down and the door lock .
Evidently the Brewsters be not at home .
Rilla run to the little barn .
It , too , be lock .
No other refuge present itself .
The bare whitewash little house have not even a veranda or porch .
It be almost dark now and her plight seem desperate .
`` I 'm go to get in if I have to break a window , '' say Rilla resolutely .
`` Hannah would want me to do that .
She 'd never get over it if she hear I come to her house for refuge in a thunderstorm and could n't get in . ''
Luckily she do not have to go to the length of actual housebreaking .
The kitchen window go up quite easily .
Rilla lift Jims in and scramble through herself , just a the storm break in good earnest .

Opening off it on one side be a trim , nicely furnished parlour , and on the other a pantry , which prove to be well stock .
`` I 'm go to make myself at home , '' say Rilla .
`` I know that be just what Hannah would want me to do .
I 'll get a little snack for Jims and me , and then if the rain continue and nobody come home I 'll just go upstairs to the spare room and go to bed .
There be nothing like act sensibly in an emergency .
If I have not be a goose when I saw Jims fall off the train I 'd have rush back into the car and get some one to stop it .

`` I ca n't be dream her . ''

`` No , '' say the big woman , speak for the first time , `` this place belong to u .
We buy it from the Brewsters last fall .
They move to Greenvale .
Our name be Chapley . ''
Poor Rilla fell back on her pillow , quite overcome .

Mrs. Brewster be a friend of mine .
I be Rilla Blythe -- Dr. Blythe 's daughter from Glen St. Mary .

`` A likely story , '' say the man .
`` We be n't bear yesterday , '' add the woman .
Madam Black-and-White do n't say anything ; but when the other two make their pretty speech she double up in a silent convulsion of mirth , shake her head from side to side and beat the air with her hand .
Rilla , stung by the disagreeable attitude of the Chapleys , regain her self-possession and lose her temper .

`` Ai n't it funny ? ''
she say .
`` I mostly let them run the length of their tether , but sometimes I have to pull them up , and then I do it with a jerk .
They do n't dast aggravate me , because I 've get considerable hard cash , and they 're afraid I wo n't leave it all to them .
Neither I will .
I 'll leave 'em some , but some I wo n't , just to vex 'em .
I have n't make up my mind where I will leave it but I 'll have to , soon , for at eighty a body be live on borrowed time .

That 's a handsome child you have there .
Is he your brother ? ''
`` No , he 's a little war-baby I 've be take care of , because his mother die and his father be overseas , '' answer Rilla in a subdued tone .
`` War-baby !
Humph !
Well , I 'd better skin out before he wake up or he 'll likely start cry .
Children do n't like me -- never do .
I ca n't recollect any youngster ever come near me of it own accord .
Never have any of my own .
Amelia be my step-daughter .
Well , it 's save me a world of bother .
If kid do n't like me I do n't like them , so that 's an even score .
But that certainly be a handsome child . ''
Jims choose this moment for wake up .
He open his big brown eye and look at Mrs. Matilda Pitman unblinkingly .
Then he sit up , dimple deliciously , point to her and say solemnly to Rilla , `` Pwitty lady , Willa , pwitty lady . ''
Mrs. Matilda Pitman smile .
Even eighty-odd be sometimes vulnerable in vanity .
`` I 've hear that child and fool tell the truth , '' she say .
`` I be use to compliment when I be young -- but they 're scarcer when you get a far along a I be .
I have n't have one for year .
It taste good .
I s ` pose now , you monkey , you would n't give me a kiss . ''
Then Jims do a quite surprising thing .
He be not a demonstrative youngster and be chary with kiss even to the Ingleside people .
But without a word he stand up in bed , his plump little body encase only in his undershirt , run to the footboard , fling his arm about Mrs. Matilda Pitman 's neck , and give her a bear hug , accompany by three or four hearty , ungrudging smack .
`` Jims , '' protest Rilla , aghast at this liberty .
`` You leave him be , '' order Mrs. Matilda Pitman , set her bonnet straight .
`` Laws I like to see some one that be n't skeered of me .
Everybody be -- you be , though you 're try to hide it .
And why ?
Of course Robert and Amelia be because I make 'em skeered on purpose .
But folk always be -- no matter how civil I be to them .
Are you go to keep this child ? ''
`` I 'm afraid not .
His father be come home before long . ''
`` Is he any good -- the father , I mean ? ''
`` Well -- he 's kind and nice -- but he 's poor -- and I 'm afraid he always will be , '' falter Rilla .
`` I see -- shiftless -- ca n't make or keep .
Well , I 'll see -- I 'll see .
I have an idea .
It 's a good idea , and besides it will make Robert and Amelia squirm .
That 's it main merit in my eye , though I like that child , mind you , because he ai n't skeered of me .
He 's worth some bother .
Now , you get dress , a I say before , and come down when you 're good and ready . ''
Rilla be stiff and sore after her tumble and walk of the night before but she be not long in dress herself and Jims .
When she go down to the kitchen she find a smoking hot breakfast on the table .
Mr. Chapley be nowhere in sight and Mrs. Chapley be cut bread with a sulky air .
Mrs. Matilda Pitman be sit in an armchair , knit a grey army sock .
She still wear her bonnet and her triumphant expression .
`` Set right in , dear , and make a good breakfast , '' she say .
`` I be not hungry , '' say Rilla almost pleadingly .
`` I do n't think I can eat anything .
And it be time I be start for the station .
The morning train will soon be along .
Please excuse me and let u go -- I 'll take a piece of bread and butter for Jims . ''
Mrs. Matilda Pitman shake a knitting-needle playfully at Rilla .
`` Sit down and take your breakfast , '' she say .
`` Mrs. Matilda Pitman command you .
Everybody obey Mrs. Matilda Pitman -- even Robert and Amelia .
You must obey her too . ''
Rilla do obey her .
She sit down and , such be the influence of Mrs. Matilda Pitman 's mesmeric eye , she eat a tolerable breakfast .
The obedient Amelia never speak ; Mrs. Matilda Pitman do not speak either ; but she knit furiously and chuckle .
When Rilla have finish , Mrs. Matilda Pitman roll up her sock .
`` Now you can go if you want to , '' she say , `` but you do n't have to go .
You can stay here as long a you want to and I 'll make Amelia cook your meal for you . ''
The independent Miss Blythe , whom a certain clique of Junior Red Cross girl accuse of be domineering and `` bossy , '' be thoroughly cow .
`` Thank you , '' she say meekly , `` but we must really go . ''
`` Well , then , '' say Mrs. Matilda Pitman , throw open the door , `` your conveyance be ready for you .
I tell Robert he must hitch up and drive you to the station .
I enjoy make Robert do thing .
It 's almost the only sport I have leave .
I 'm over eighty and most thing have lose their flavour except boss Robert . ''
Robert sit before the door on the front seat of a trim , double-seated , rubber-tired buggy .
He must have hear every word his mother-in-law say but he give no sign .
`` I do wish , '' say Rilla , pluck up what little spirit she have leave , `` that you would let me -- oh -- ah -- '' then she quail again before Mrs. Matilda Pitman 's eye -- `` recompense you for -- for -- '' `` Mrs. Matilda Pitman say before -- and mean it -- that she do n't take pay for entertain stranger , nor let other people where she live do it , much a their natural meanness would like to do it .
You go along to town and do n't forget to call the next time you come this way .
Do n't be scar .
Not that you be scar of much , I reckon , consider the way you sass Robert back this morning .
I like your spunk .
Most girl nowadays be such timid , skeery creeturs .
When I be a girl I be n't afraid of nothing nor nobody .
Mind you take good care of that boy .
He ai n't any common child .
And make Robert drive round all the puddle in the road .
I wo n't have that new buggy splash . ''
As they drive away Jims throw kiss at Mrs. Matilda Pitman as long a he could see her , and Mrs. Matilda Pitman wave her sock back at him .
Robert speak no word , either good or bad , all the way to the station , but he remember the puddle .
When Rilla get out at the side she thank him courteously .
The only response she get be a grunt a Robert turn his horse and start for home .
`` Well '' -- Rilla draw a long breath -- `` I must try to get back into Rilla Blythe again .
I 've be somebody else these past few hour -- I do n't know just who -- some creation of that extraordinary old person 's .
I believe she hypnotize me .
What an adventure this will be to write the boy . ''
And then she sigh .
Bitter remembrance come that there be only Jerry , Ken , Carl and Shirley to write it to now .
Jem -- who would have appreciate Mrs. Matilda Pitman keenly -- where be Jem ?
CHAPTER XXXII WORD FROM JEM 4th August 1918 `` It be four year tonight since the dance at the lighthouse -- four year of war .
It seem like three time four .
I be fifteen then .
I be nineteen now .
I expect that these past four year would be the most delightful year of my life and they have be year of war -- year of fear and grief and worry -- but I humbly hope , of a little growth in strength and character as well .
`` Today I be go through the hall and I hear mother say something to father about me .
I do n't mean to listen -- I could n't help hear her a I go along the hall and upstairs -- so perhaps that be why I hear what listener be say never to hear -- something good of myself .
And because it be mother who say it I 'm go to write it here in my journal , for my comforting when day of discouragement come upon me , in which I feel that I be vain and selfish and weak and that there be no good thing in me . ''
` Rilla have develop in a wonderful fashion these past four year .
She use to be such an irresponsible young creature .
She have change into a capable , womanly girl and she be such a comfort to me .
Nan and Di have grow a little away from me -- they have be so little at home -- but Rilla have grow closer and close to me .
We be chum .
I do n't see how I could have get through these terrible year without her , Gilbert . '
`` There , that be just what mother say -- and I feel glad -- and sorry -- and proud -- and humble !
It 's beautiful to have my mother think that about me -- but I do n't deserve it quite .
I 'm not as good and strong a all that .
There be heap of time when I have felt cross and impatient and woeful and despairing .
It be mother and Susan who have be this family 's backbone .
But I have help a little , I believe , and I be so glad and thankful .
`` The war news have be good right along .
The French and Americans be push the Germans back and back and back .
Sometimes I be afraid it be too good to last -- after nearly four year of disaster one have a feeling that this constant success be unbelievable .
We do n't rejoice noisily over it .
Susan keep the flag up but we go softly .
The price pay have be too high for jubilation .
We be just thankful that it have not be pay in vain .
`` No word have come from Jem .
We hope -- because we dare not do anything else .
But there be hour when we all feel -- though we never say so -- that such hoping be foolishness .
These hour come more and more frequently a the week go by .
And we may never know .
That be the most terrible thought of all .
I wonder how Faith be bear it .
To judge from her letter she have never for a moment give up hope , but she must have have her dark hour of doubt like the rest of u . ''
20th August 1918 `` The Canadians have be in action again and Mr. Meredith have a cable today say that Carl have be slightly wound and be in the hospital .
It do not say where the wound be , which be unusual , and we all feel worried .
There be news of a fresh victory every day now . ''
30th August 1918 `` The Merediths have a letter from Carl today .
His wound be `` only a slight one '' -- but it be in his right eye and the sight be go for ever ! ''
` One eye be enough to watch bug with , ' Carl write cheerfully .
And we know it might have be oh so much bad !
If it have be both eye !
But I cry all the afternoon after I saw Carl 's letter .
Those beautiful , fearless blue eye of his !
`` There be one comfort -- he will not have to go back to the front .
He be come home as soon a he be out of the hospital -- the first of our boy to return .
When will the others come ?
`` And there be one who will never come .
At least we will not see him if he do .
But , oh , I think he will be there -- when our Canadian soldier return there will be a shadow army with them -- the army of the fallen .
We will not see them -- but they will be there ! ''
1st September 1918 `` Mother and I go into Charlottetown yesterday to see the move picture , `` Hearts of the World . ''
I make an awful goose of myself -- father will never stop tease me about it for the rest of my life .
But it all seem so horribly real -- and I be so intensely interested that I forget everything but the scene I saw enact before my eye .
And then , quite near the last come a terribly excite one .
The heroine be struggle with a horrible German soldier who be try to drag her away .
I know she have a knife -- I have see her hide it , to have it in readiness -- and I could n't understand why she do n't produce it and finish the brute .
I think she must have forget it , and just at the tense moment of the scene I lose my head altogether .
I just stand right up on my foot in that crowded house and shriek at the top of my voice -- ` The knife be in your stocking -- the knife be in your stocking ! '
`` I create a sensation !
`` The funny part be , that just a I say it , the girl do snatch out the knife and stab the soldier with it !
`` Everybody in the house laugh .
I come to my sens and fell back in my seat , overcome with mortification .
Mother be shake with laughter .
I could have shake her .
Why have n't she pull me down and choke me before I have make such an idiot of myself .
She protest that there be n't time .
`` Fortunately the house be dark , and I do n't believe there be anybody there who know me .
And I think I be become sensible and self-controlled and womanly !
It be plain I have some distance to go yet before I attain that devoutly desire consummation . ''
20th September 1918 `` In the east Bulgaria have ask for peace , and in the west the British have smash the Hindenburg line ; and right here in Glen St. Mary little Bruce Meredith have do something that I think wonderful -- wonderful because of the love behind it .
Mrs. Meredith be here tonight and tell u about it -- and mother and I cry , and Susan get up and clatter the thing about the stove .
`` Bruce always love Jem very devotedly , and the child have never forget him in all these year .
He have be a faithful in his way a Dog Monday be in his .
We have always tell him that Jem would come back .
But it seem that he be in Carter Flagg 's store last night and he hear his Uncle Norman flatly declare that Jem Blythe would never come back and that the Ingleside folk might as well give up hop he would .
Bruce go home and cry himself to sleep .
This morning his mother saw him go out of the yard , with a very sorrowful and determined look , carry his pet kitten .
She do n't think much more about it until later on he come in , with the most tragic little face , and tell her , his little body shake with sob , that he have drown Stripey . ''
` Why do you do that ? '
Mrs. Meredith exclaim . ''
` To bring Jem back , ' sob Bruce . '
I think if I sacrifice Stripey God would send Jem back .
So I drownded him -- and , oh mother , it be awful hard -- but surely God will send Jem back now , 'cause Stripey be the dear thing I have .
I just tell God I would give Him Stripey if He would send Jem back .
And He will , wo n't He , mother ? '
`` Mrs. Meredith do n't know what to say to the poor child .
She just could not tell him that perhaps his sacrifice would n't bring Jem back -- that God do n't work that way .
She tell him that he must n't expect it right away -- that perhaps it would be quite a long time yet before Jem come back .
`` But Bruce say , ` It ought n't to take long 'n a week , mother .
Oh , mother , Stripey be such a nice little cat .
He purr so pretty .
Do n't you think God ought to like him enough to let u have Jem ? ''
`` Mr. Meredith be worry about the effect on Bruce 's faith in God , and Mrs. Meredith be worry about the effect on Bruce himself if his hope be n't fulfil .
And I feel a if I must cry every time I think of it .
It be so splendid -- and sad -- and beautiful .
The dear devote little fellow !
He worship that kitten .
And if it all go for nothing -- a so many sacrifice seem to go for nothing -- he will be brokenhearted , for he be n't old enough to understand that God do n't answer our prayer just a we hope -- and do n't make bargain with u when we yield something we love up to Him . ''
24th September 1918 `` I have be kneel at my window in the moonshine for a long time , just thank God over and over again .
The joy of last night and today have be so great that it seem half pain -- a if our heart be n't big enough to hold it .
`` Last night I be sit here in my room at eleven o'clock write a letter to Shirley .
Every one else be in bed , except father , who be out .
I hear the telephone ring and I run out to the hall to answer it , before it should waken mother .
It be long-distance call , and when I answer it say ` This be the telegraph Company 's office in Charlottetown .
There be an overseas cable for Dr. Blythe . '
`` I think of Shirley -- my heart stand still -- and then I hear him say , ` It 's from Holland . '
`` The message be , ` Just arrive .
Escaped from Germany .
Quite well .
Writing .
James Blythe . '
`` I do n't faint or fall or scream .
I do n't feel glad or surprised .
I do n't feel anything .
I felt numb , just a I do when I hear Walter have enlist .
I hang up the receiver and turn round .
Mother be stand in her doorway .
She wear her old rise kimono , and her hair be hang down her back in a long thick braid , and her eye be shin .
She look just like a young girl . ''
` There be word from Jem ? '
she say .
`` How do she know ?
I have n't say a word at the phone except ` Yes -- yes -- yes . '
She say she do n't know how she know , but she do know .
She be awake and she hear the ring and she know that there be word from Jem . ''
` He 's alive -- he 's well -- he 's in Holland , ' I say .
`` Mother come out into the hall and say , ' I must get your father on the ` phone and tell him .
He be in the Upper Glen . '
`` She be very calm and quiet -- not a bit like I would have expect her to be .
But then I be n't either .
I go and wake up Gertrude and Susan and tell them .
Susan say ` Thank God , ' firstly , and secondly she say ` Did I not tell you Dog Monday know ? '
and thirdly , ` I 'll go down and make a cup of tea ' -- and she stalk down in her nightdress to make it .
She do make it -- and make mother and Gertrude drink it -- but I go back to my room and shut my door and lock it , and I kneel by my window and cry -- just a Gertrude do when her great news come .
`` I think I know at last exactly what I shall feel like on the resurrection morning . ''
4th October 1918 `` Today Jem 's letter come .
It have be in the house only six hour and it be almost read to piece .
The post-mistress told everybody in the Glen it have come , and everybody come up to hear the news .
`` Jem be badly wound in the thigh -- and he be pick up and take to prison , so delirious with fever that he do n't know what be happen to him or where he be .
It be week before he come to his sens and be able to write .
Then he do write -- but it never come .
He be n't treat at all badly at his camp -- only the food be poor .
He have nothing to eat but a little black bread and boil turnip and now and then a little soup with black pea in it .
And we sit down every one of those day to three good square luxurious meal !
He write u as often a he could but he be afraid we be not get his letter because no reply come .
As soon a he be strong enough he try to escape , but be catch and bring back ; a month later he and a comrade make another attempt and succeed in reach Holland .
`` Jem ca n't come home right away .
He be n't quite so well a his cable say , for his wound have not heal properly and he have to go into a hospital in England for further treatment .
But he say he will be all right eventually , and we know he be safe and will be back home sometime , and oh , the difference it make in everything !
`` I have a letter from Jim Anderson today , too .
He have marry an English girl , get his discharge , and be come right home to Canada with his bride .
I do n't know whether to be glad or sorry .
It will depend on what kind of a woman she be .
I have a second letter also of a somewhat mysterious tenor .
It be from a Charlottetown lawyer , ask me to go in to see him at my early convenience in regard to a certain matter connect with the estate of the ` late Mrs. Matilda Pitman . '
`` I read a notice of Mrs. Pitman 's death -- from heart failure -- in the Enterprise a few week ago .
I wonder if this summons have anything to do with Jims . ''
5th October 1918 `` I go into town this morning and have an interview with Mrs. Pitman 's lawyer -- a little thin , wispy man , who speak of his late client with such a profound respect that it be evident that he a be much under her thumb a Robert and Amelia be .
He draw up a new will for her a short time before her death .
She be worth thirty thousand dollar , the bulk of which be leave to Amelia Chapley .
But she leave five thousand to me in trust for Jims .
The interest be to be use a I see fit for his education , and the principal be to be pay over to him on his twentieth birthday .
Certainly Jims be bear lucky .
I save him from slow extinction at the hand of Mrs. Conover -- Mary Vance save him from death by diptheritic croup -- his star save him when he fell off the train .
And he tumble not only into a clump of bracken , but right into this nice little legacy .
`` Evidently , a Mrs. Matilda Pitman say , and a I have always believe , he be no common child and he have no common destiny in store for him .
`` At all event he be provide for , and in such a fashion that Jim Anderson ca n't squander his inheritance if he want to .
Now , if the new English stepmother be only a good sort I shall feel quite easy about the future of my war-baby .
`` I wonder what Robert and Amelia think of it .
I fancy they will nail down their window when they leave home after this ! ''
CHAPTER XXXIII VICTORY !
`` A day ` of chill wind and gloomy sky , ' '' Rilla quote one Sunday afternoon -- the sixth of October to be exact .
It be so cold that they have light a fire in the living-room and the merry little flame be do their best to counteract the outside dourness .
`` It 's more like November than October -- November be such an ugly month . ''
Cousin Sophia be there , have again forgiven Susan , and Mrs. Martin Clow , who be not visit on Sunday but have drop in to borrow Susan 's cure for rheumatism -- that be cheap than get one from the doctor .
`` I 'm afeared we 're go to have an airly winter , '' forebode Cousin Sophia .
`` The muskrat be build awful big house round the pond , and that 's a sign that never fail .
Dear me , how that child have grow ! ''
Cousin Sophia sigh again , a if it be an unhappy circumstance that a child should grow .
`` When do you expect his father ? ''
`` Next week , '' say Rilla .
`` Well , I hope the stepmother wo n't abuse the pore child , '' sigh Cousin Sophia , `` but I have my doubt -- I have my doubt .
Anyhow , he 'll be sure to feel the difference between his usage here and what he 'll get anywhere else .
You 've spoil him so , Rilla , wait on him hand and foot the way you 've always do . ''
Rilla smile and press her cheek to Jims ' curl .
She know sweet-tempered , sunny , little Jims be not spoil .
Nevertheless her heart be anxious behind her smile .
She , too , think much about the new Mrs. Anderson and wonder uneasily what she would be like .
`` I ca n't give Jims up to a woman who wo n't love him , '' she think rebelliously .
`` I b ` lieve it 's go to rain , '' say Cousin Sophia .
`` We have have an awful lot of rain this fall already .
It 's go to make it awful hard for people to get their root in .
It be n't so in my young day .
We gin ` rally have beautiful Octobers then .
But the season be altogether different now from what they use to be . ''
Clear across Cousin Sophia 's doleful voice cut the telephone bell .
Gertrude Oliver answer it .
`` Yes -- what ?
What ?
Is it true -- be it official ?
Thank you -- thank you . ''
Gertrude turn and face the room dramatically , her dark eye flash , her dark face flush with feeling .
All at once the sun break through the thick cloud and pour through the big crimson maple outside the window .
Its reflect glow envelop her in a weird immaterial flame .
She look like a priestess perform some mystic , splendid rite .
`` Germany and Austria be sue for peace , '' she say .
Rilla go crazy for a few minute .
She spring up and dance around the room , clap her hand , laugh , cry .
`` Sit down , child , '' say Mrs. Clow , who never get excite over anything , and so have miss a tremendous amount of trouble and delight in her journey through life .
`` Oh , '' cry Rilla , `` I have walk the floor for hour in despair and anxiety in these past four year .
Now let me walk in joy .
It be worth living long dreary year for this minute , and it would be worth live them again just to look back to it .
Susan , let 's run up the flag -- and we must phone the news to every one in the Glen . ''
`` Can we have as much sugar a we want to now ? ''
ask Jims eagerly .
It be a never-to-be-forgotten afternoon .
As the news spread excite people run about the village and dash up to Ingleside .
The Merediths come over and stay to supper and everybody talk and nobody listen .
Cousin Sophia try to protest that Germany and Austria be not to be trust and it be all part of a plot , but nobody pay the least attention to her .
`` This Sunday make up for that one in March , '' say Susan .
`` I wonder , '' say Gertrude dreamily , apart to Rilla , `` if thing wo n't seem rather flat and insipid when peace really come .
After be feed for four year on horror and fear , terrible revers , amaze victory , wo n't anything less be tame and uninteresting ?
How strange -- and bless -- and dull it will be not to dread the come of the mail every day . ''
`` We must dread it for a little while yet , I suppose , '' say Rilla .
`` Peace wo n't come -- ca n't come -- for some week yet .
And in those week dreadful thing may happen .
My excitement be over .
We have win the victory -- but oh , what a price we have pay ! ''
`` Not too high a price for freedom , '' say Gertrude softly .
`` Do you think it be , Rilla ? ''
`` No , '' say Rilla , under her breath .
She be see a little white cross on a battlefield of France .
`` No -- not if those of u who live will show ourselves worthy of it -- if we ` keep faith . ' ''
`` We will keep faith , '' say Gertrude .
She rise suddenly .
A silence fell around the table , and in the silence Gertrude repeat Walter 's famous poem `` The Piper . ''
When she finish Mr. Meredith stand up and hold up his glass .
`` Let u drink , '' he say , `` to the silent army -- to the boy who follow when the Piper summon .
` For our tomorrow they give their today ' -- theirs be the victory ! ''
CHAPTER XXXIV MR. HYDE GOES TO HIS OWN PLACE AND SUSAN TAKES A HONEYMOON Early in November Jims leave Ingleside .
Rilla saw him go with many tear but a heart free from bode .
Mrs. Jim Anderson , Number Two , be such a nice little woman that one be rather inclined to wonder at the luck which bestow her on Jim .
She be rosy-faced and blue-eyed and wholesome , with the roundness and trigness of a geranium leaf .
Rilla saw at first glance that she be to be trust with Jims .
`` I 'm fond of child , miss , '' she say heartily .
`` I 'm use to them -- I 've leave six little brother and sister behind me .
Jims be a dear child and I must say you 've do wonder in bring him up so healthy and handsome .
I 'll be as good to him a if he be my own , miss .
And I 'll make Jim toe the line all right .
He 's a good worker -- all he need be some one to keep him at it , and to take charge of his money .
We 've rent a little farm just out of the village , and we 're go to settle down there .
Jim want to stay in England but I say ` No . '
I hanker to try a new country and I 've always think Canada would suit me . ''
`` I 'm so glad you be go to live near u .
You 'll let Jims come here often , wo n't you ?
I love him dearly . ''
`` No doubt you do , miss , for a lovabler child I never do see .
We understand , Jim and me , what you 've do for him , and you wo n't find u ungrateful .
He can come here whenever you want him and I 'll always be glad of any advice from you about his bring up .
He be more your baby than anyone else 's I should say , and I 'll see that you get your fair share of him , miss . ''
So Jims go away -- with the soup tureen , though not in it .
Then the news of the Armistice come , and even Glen St. Mary go mad .
That night the village have a bonfire , and burn the Kaiser in effigy .
The fishing village boy turn out and burn all the sandhills off in one grand glorious conflagration that extend for seven mile .
Up at Ingleside Rilla run laugh to her room .
`` Now I 'm go to do a most unladylike and inexcusable thing , '' she say , a she pull her green velvet hat out of it box .
`` I 'm go to kick this hat about the room until it be without form and void ; and I shall never as long a I live wear anything of that shade of green again . ''
`` You 've certainly keep your vow pluckily , '' laugh Miss Oliver .
`` It be n't pluck -- it be sheer obstinacy -- I 'm rather ashamed of it , '' say Rilla , kick joyously .
`` I want to show mother .
It 's mean to want to show your own mother -- most unfilial conduct !
But I have show her .
And I 've show myself a few thing !
Oh , Miss Oliver , just for one moment I 'm really feel quite young again -- young and frivolous and silly .
Did I ever say November be an ugly month ?
Why it 's the most beautiful month in the whole year .
Listen to the bell ring in Rainbow Valley !
I never hear them so clearly .
They 're ring for peace -- and new happiness -- and all the dear , sweet , sane , homey thing that we can have again now , Miss Oliver .
Not that I be sane just now -- I do n't pretend to be .
The whole world be have a little crazy spell today .
Soon we 'll sober down -- and ` keep faith ' -- and begin to build up our new world .
But just for today let 's be mad and glad . ''
Susan come in from the outdoor sunlight look supremely satisfy .
`` Mr. Hyde be go , '' she announce .
`` Gone !
Do you mean he be dead , Susan ? ''
`` No , Mrs. Dr. dear , that beast be not dead .
But you will never see him again .
I feel sure of that . ''
`` Do n't be so mysterious , Susan .
What have happen to him ? ''
`` Well , Mrs. Dr. dear , he be sit out on the back step this afternoon .
It be just after the news come that the Armistice have be sign and he be look his Hydest .
I can assure you he be an awesome look beast .
All at once , Mrs. Dr. dear , Bruce Meredith come around the corner of the kitchen walk on his stilt .
He have be learn to walk on them lately and come over to show me how well he could do it .
Mr. Hyde just take a look and one bound carry him over the yard fence .
Then he go tear through the maple grove in great leap with his ear lay back .
You never saw a creature so terrified , Mrs. Dr. dear .
He have never return . ''
`` Oh , he 'll come back , Susan , probably chasten in spirit by his fright . ''
`` We will see , Mrs. Dr. dear -- we will see .
Remember , the Armistice have be sign .
And that remind me that Whiskers-on-the-moon have a paralytic stroke last night .
I be not say it be a judgment on him , because I be not in the counsel of the Almighty , but one can have one 's own thought about it .

CHAPTER XXXV `` RILLA-MY-RILLA ! ''
Carl Meredith and Miller Douglas come home just before Christmas and Glen St. Mary meet them at the station with a brass band borrow from Lowbridge and speech of home manufacture .

Thousands of train have Dog Monday meet and never have the boy he wait and watch for return .

A black-and-yellow streak shot past the station agent .
Dog Monday stiff ?
Dog Monday rheumatic ?
Dog Monday old ?
Never believe it .
Dog Monday be a young pup , go clean mad with rejuvenate joy .

Jem Blythe have come home .

`` One night when Jem and I be talk thing over in Rainbow Valley , I ask him if he have ever felt afraid at the front .
`` Jem laugh . ''
` Afraid !

CHAPTER I .

We do not know her at all under that name .
We know only that a cousin , Sara Stanley , whose mother , our Aunt Felicity , be dead , be live down on the Island with Uncle Roger and Aunt Olivia King , on a farm adjoin the old King homestead in Carlisle .
We suppose we should get acquaint with her when we reach there , and we have an idea , from Aunt Olivia 's letter to father , that she would be quite a jolly creature .
Further than that we do not think about her .
We be more interested in Felicity and Cecily and Dan , who live on the homestead and would therefore be our roofmates for a season .

We be fare forth on a long road ; and , though we have some idea what would be at the end of it , there be enough glamour of the unknown about it to lend a wonderful charm to our speculation concern it .
We be delight at the thought of see father 's old home , and living among the haunt of his boyhood .
He have talk so much to u about it , and describe it scene so often and so minutely , that he have inspire u with some of his own deep-seated affection for it -- an affection that have never wan in all his year of exile .
We have a vague feeling that we , somehow , belong there , in that cradle of our family , though we have never see it .
We have always look forward eagerly to the promise day when father would take u `` down home , '' to the old house with the spruce behind it and the famous `` King orchard '' before it -- when we might ramble in `` Uncle Stephen 's Walk , '' drink from the deep well with the Chinese roof over it , stand on `` the Pulpit Stone , '' and eat apple from our `` birthday tree . His firm ask him to go to Rio de Janeiro that spring to take charge of their new branch there .

CHAPTER II .
A QUEEN OF HEARTS I waken shortly after sunrise .

We know all about it , from father 's description , and in fancy we have roam in it many a time and oft .
It be now nearly sixty year since it have have it beginning , when Grandfather King bring his bride home .
Before the wedding he have fence off the big south meadow that slop to the sun ; it be the fine , most fertile field on the farm , and the neighbour tell young Abraham King that he would raise many a fine crop of wheat in that meadow .
Abraham King smile and , be a man of few word , say nothing ; but in his mind he have a vision of the year to be , and in that vision he saw , not ripple acre of harvest gold , but great , leafy avenue of wide-spreading tree lade with fruit to gladden the eye of child and grandchild yet unborn .
It be a vision to develop slowly into fulfilment .
Grandfather King be in no hurry .

They have fourteen child in all , and each child have it `` birth tree . ''
Every family festival be commemorate in like fashion , and every beloved visitor who spend a night under their roof be expect to plant a tree in the orchard .
So it come to pass that every tree in it be a fair green monument to some love or delight of the vanished year .
And each grandchild have it tree , there , also , set out by grandfather when the tidings of it birth reach him ; not always an apple tree -- perhaps it be a plum , or cherry or pear .
But it be always know by the name of the person for whom , or by whom , it be plant ; and Felix and I know as much about `` Aunt Felicity 's pear , '' and `` Aunt Julia 's cherry , '' and `` Uncle Alec 's apple , '' and the `` Rev . Mr. Scott 's plum , '' a if we have be bear and breed among them .
And now we have come to the orchard ; it be before u ; we have only to open that little whitewashed gate in the hedge and we might find ourselves in it storied domain .
But before we reach the gate we glance to our left , along the grassy , spruce-bordered lane which lead over to Uncle Roger 's ; and at the entrance of that lane we saw a girl standing , with a gray cat at her foot .
She lift her hand and beckon blithely to u ; and , the orchard forgotten , we follow her summons .
For we know that this must be the Story Girl ; and in that gay and graceful gesture be an allurement not to be gainsay or deny .
We look at her a we draw near with such interest that we forget to feel shy .
No , she be not pretty .
She be tall for her fourteen year , slim and straight ; around her long , white face -- rather too long and too white -- fell sleek , dark-brown curl , tie above either ear with rosette of scarlet ribbon .
Her large , curve mouth be as red a a poppy , and she have brilliant , almond-shaped , hazel eye ; but we do not think her pretty .
Then she speak ; she say , `` Good morning . ''
Never have we hear a voice like hers .
Never , in all my life since , have I hear such a voice .
I can not describe it .
I might say it be clear ; I might say it be sweet ; I might say it be vibrant and far-reaching and bell-like ; all this would be true , but it would give you no real idea of the peculiar quality which make the Story Girl 's voice what it be .
If voice have colour , hers would have be like a rainbow .
It make word LIVE .
Whatever she say become a breathing entity , not a mere verbal statement or utterance .
Felix and I be too young to understand or analyze the impression it make upon u ; but we instantly felt at her greeting that it WAS a good morning -- a surpassingly good morning -- the very best morning that have ever happen in this most excellent of world .
`` You be Felix and Beverley , '' she go on , shake our hand with an air of frank comradeship , which be very different from the shy , feminine advance of Felicity and Cecily .
From that moment we be as good friend a if we have know each other for a hundred year .
`` I be glad to see you .
I be so disappointed I could n't go over last night .
I get up early this morning , though , for I felt sure you would be up early , too , and that you 'd like to have me tell you about thing .
I can tell thing so much good than Felicity or Cecily .
Do you think Felicity be VERY pretty ? ''
`` She 's the pretty girl I ever saw , '' I say enthusiastically , remember that Felicity have call me handsome .
`` The boy all think so , '' say the Story Girl , not , I fancy , quite well pleased .
`` And I suppose she be .
She be a splendid cook , too , though she be only twelve .
I ca n't cook .
I be try to learn , but I do n't make much progress .
Aunt Olivia say I have n't enough natural gumption ever to be a cook ; but I 'd love to be able to make a good cake and pie a Felicity can make .
But then , Felicity be stupid .
It 's not ill-natured of me to say that .
It 's just the truth , and you 'd soon find it out for yourselves .
I like Felicity very well , but she IS stupid .
Cecily be ever so much cleverer .
Cecily 's a dear .
So be Uncle Alec ; and Aunt Janet be pretty nice , too . ''
`` What be Aunt Olivia like ? ''
ask Felix .
`` Aunt Olivia be very pretty .
She be just like a pansy -- all velvety and purply and goldy . ''
Felix and I SAW , somewhere inside of our head , a velvet and purple and gold pansy-woman , just a the Story Girl speak .
`` But be she NICE ? ''
I ask .
That be the main question about grown-ups .
Their look matter little to u .
`` She be lovely .
But she be twenty-nine , you know .
That 's pretty old .
She do n't bother me much .
Aunt Janet say that I 'd have no bring up at all , if it be n't for her .
Aunt Olivia say child should just be let COME up -- that everything else be settle for them long before they be bear .
I do n't understand that .
Do you ? ''
No , we do not .
But it be our experience that grown-ups have a habit of say thing hard to understand .
`` What be Uncle Roger like ? ''
be our next question .
`` Well , I like Uncle Roger , '' say the Story Girl meditatively .
`` He be big and jolly .
But he tease people too much .
You ask him a serious question and you get a ridiculous answer .
He hardly ever scold or get cross , though , and THAT be something .
He be an old bachelor . ''
`` Does n't he ever mean to get marry ? ''
ask Felix .
`` I do n't know .
Aunt Olivia wish he would , because she 's tired keep house for him , and she want to go to Aunt Julia in California .
But she say he 'll never get marry , because he be look for perfection , and when he find her she wo n't have HIM . ''
By this time we be all sit down on the gnarled root of the spruce , and the big gray cat come over and make friend with u .
He be a lordly animal , with a silver-gray coat beautifully mark with darker stripe .
With such colour most cat would have have white or silver foot ; but he have four black paw and a black nose .
Such point give him an air of distinction , and mark him out a quite different from the common or garden variety of cat .
He seem to be a cat with a tolerably good opinion of himself , and his response to our advance be slightly ting with condescension .
`` This be n't Topsy , be it ? ''
I ask .
I know at once that the question be a foolish one .
Topsy , the cat of which father have talk , have flourish thirty year before , and all her nine life could scarcely have last so long .
`` No , but it be Topsy 's great-great-great-great-grandson , '' say the Story Girl gravely .
`` His name be Paddy and he be my own particular cat .
We have barn cat , but Paddy never associate with them .
I be very good friend with all cat .
They be so sleek and comfortable and dignified .
And it be so easy to make them happy .
Oh , I 'm so glad you boy have come to live here .
Nothing ever happen here , except day , so we have to make our own good time .
We be short of boy before -- only Dan and Peter to four girl . ''
`` FOUR girl ?
Oh , yes , Sara Ray .
Felicity mention her .
What be she like ?
Where do she live ? ''
`` Just down the hill .
You ca n't see the house for the spruce bush .
Sara be a nice girl .
She 's only eleven , and her mother be dreadfully strict .
She never allow Sara to read a single story .
JUST you fancy !
Sara 's conscience be always trouble her for do thing she 's sure her mother wo n't approve , but it never prevent her from do them .
It only spoil her fun .
Uncle Roger say that a mother who wo n't let you do anything , and a conscience that wo n't let you enjoy anything be an awful combination , and he do n't wonder Sara be pale and thin and nervous .
But , between you and me , I believe the real reason be that her mother do n't give her half enough to eat .
Not that she 's mean , you know -- but she think it be n't healthy for child to eat much , or anything but certain thing .
Is n't it fortunate we be n't bear into that sort of a family ? ''
`` I think it 's awfully lucky we be all bear into the same family , '' Felix remark .
`` Is n't it ?
I 've often think so .
And I 've often think what a dreadful thing it would have be if Grandfather and Grandmother King have never get marry to each other .
I do n't suppose there would have be a single one of u child here at all ; or if we be , we would be part somebody else and that would be almost a bad .
When I think it all over I ca n't feel too thankful that Grandfather and Grandmother King happen to marry each other , when there be so many other people they might have marry . ''
Felix and I shiver .
We felt suddenly that we have escape a dreadful danger -- the danger of have be bear somebody else .
But it take the Story Girl to make u realize just how dreadful it be and what a terrible risk we have run year before we , or our parent either , have exist .
`` Who live over there ? ''
I ask , point to a house across the field .
`` Oh , that belong to the Awkward Man .
His name be Jasper Dale , but everybody call him the Awkward Man .
And they do say he write poetry .
He call his place Golden Milestone .
I know why , because I 've read Longfellow 's poem .
He never go into society because he be so awkward .
The girl laugh at him and he do n't like it .
I know a story about him and I 'll tell it to you sometime . ''
`` And who live in that other house ? ''
ask Felix , look over the westering valley where a little gray roof be visible among the tree .
`` Old Peg Bowen .
She 's very queer .
She live there with a lot of pet animal in winter , and in summer she roam over the country and beg her meal .
They say she be crazy .
People have always try to frighten u child into good behaviour by tell u that Peg Bowen would catch u if we do n't behave .
I 'm not so frightened of her a I once be , but I do n't think I would like to be catch by her .
Sara Ray be dreadfully scared of her .
Peter Craig say she be a witch and that he bet she 's at the bottom of it when the butter wo n't come .
But I do n't believe THAT .
Witches be so scarce nowadays .
There may be some somewhere in the world , but it 's not likely there be any here right in Prince Edward Island .
They use to be very plenty long ago .
I know some splendid witch story I 'll tell you some day .
They 'll just make your blood freeze in your vein . ''
We have n't a doubt of it .
If anybody could freeze the blood in our vein this girl with the wonderful voice could .
But it be a May morning , and our young blood be run blithely in our vein .
We suggest a visit to the orchard would be more agreeable .
`` All right .
I know story about it , too , '' she say , a we walk across the yard , follow by Paddy of the wave tail .
`` Oh , be n't you glad it be spring ?
The beauty of winter be that it make you appreciate spring . ''
The latch of the gate click under the Story Girl 's hand , and the next moment we be in the King orchard .
CHAPTER III .
LEGENDS OF THE OLD ORCHARD Outside of the orchard the grass be only begin to grow green ; but here , shelter by the spruce hedge from uncertain wind and slop to southern sun , it be already like a wonderful velvet carpet ; the leaf on the tree be begin to come out in woolly , grayish cluster ; and there be purple-pencilled white violet at the base of the Pulpit Stone .
`` It 's all just a father describe it , '' say Felix with a blissful sigh , `` and there 's the well with the Chinese roof . ''
We hurry over to it , tread on the spear of mint that be begin to shoot up about it .
It be a very deep well , and the curb be of rough , undressed stone .
Over it , the queer , pagoda-like roof , build by Uncle Stephen on his return from a voyage to China , be cover with yet leafless vine .
`` It 's so pretty , when the vine leaf out and hang down in long festoon , '' say the Story Girl .
`` The bird build their nest in it .
A pair of wild canary come here every summer .
And fern grow out between the stone of the well a far down a you can see .
The water be lovely .
Uncle Edward preach his fine sermon about the Bethlehem well where David 's soldier go to get him water , and he illustrate it by describe his old well at the homestead -- this very well -- and how in foreign land he have long for it sparkling water .
So you see it be quite famous . ''
`` There 's a cup just like the one that use to be here in father 's time , '' exclaim Felix , point to an old-fashioned shallow cup of clouded blue ware on a little shelf inside the curb .
`` It be the very same cup , '' say the Story Girl impressively .
`` Is n't it an amazing thing ?
That cup have be here for forty year , and hundred of people have drink from it , and it have never be break .
Aunt Julia drop it down the well once , but they fish it up , not hurt a bit except for that little nick in the rim .
I think it be bind up with the fortune of the King family , like the Luck of Edenhall in Longfellow 's poem .
It be the last cup of Grandmother King 's second best set .
Her best set be still complete .
Aunt Olivia have it .
You must get her to show it to you .
It 's so pretty , with red berry all over it , and the funny little pot-bellied cream jug .
Aunt Olivia never use it except on a family anniversary . ''
We take a drink from the blue cup and then go to find our birthday tree .
We be rather disappointed to find them quite large , sturdy one .
It seem to u that they should still be in the sapling stage correspond to our boyhood .
`` Your apple be lovely to eat , '' the Story Girl say to me , `` but Felix 's be only good for pie .
Those two big tree behind them be the twin ' tree -- my mother and Uncle Felix , you know .
The apple be so dead sweet that nobody but u child and the French boy can eat them .
And that tall , slender tree over there , with the branch all grow straight up , be a seedling that come up of itself , and NOBODY can eat it apple , they be so sour and bitter .
Even the pig wo n't eat them .
Aunt Janet try to make pie of them once , because she say she hat to see them go to waste .
But she never try again .
She say it be well to waste apple alone than apple and sugar too .
And then she try give them away to the French hire men , but they would n't even carry them home . ''
The Story Girl 's word fell on the morning air like pearl and diamond .
Even her preposition and conjunction have untold charm , hint at mystery and laughter and magic bound up in everything she mention .
Apple pie and sour seedling and pig become straightway invest with a glamour of romance .
`` I like to hear you talk , '' say Felix in his grave , stodgy way .
`` Everybody do , '' say the Story Girl coolly .
`` I 'm glad you like the way I talk .
But I want you to like ME , too -- AS WELL a you like Felicity and Cecily .
Not BETTER .
I want that once but I 've get over it .
I find out in Sunday School , the day the minister teach our class , that it be selfish .
But I want you to like me AS WELL . ''
`` Well , I will , for one , '' say Felix emphatically .
I think he be remember that Felicity have call him fat .
Cecily now joined u .
It appear that it be Felicity 's morning to help prepare breakfast , therefore she could not come .
We all go to Uncle Stephen 's Walk .
This be a double row of apple tree , run down the western side of the orchard .
Uncle Stephen be the first born of Abraham and Elizabeth King .
He have none of grandfather 's abide love for wood and meadow and the kindly way of the warm red earth .
Grandmother King have be a Ward , and in Uncle Stephen the blood of the seafaring race claim it own .
To sea he must go , despite the pleading and tear of a reluctant mother ; and it be from the sea he come to set out his avenue in the orchard with tree bring from a foreign land .
Then he sail away again -- and the ship be never hear of more .
The gray first come in grandmother 's brown hair in those month of wait .
The , for the first time , the orchard hear the sound of weep and be consecrate by a sorrow .
`` When the blossom come out it 's wonderful to walk here , '' say the Story Girl .
`` It 's like a dream of fairyland -- a if you be walk in a king 's palace .
The apple be delicious , and in winter it 's a splendid place for coast . ''
From the Walk we go to the Pulpit Stone -- a huge gray boulder , as high a a man 's head , in the southeastern corner .
It be straight and smooth in front , but slop down in natural step behind , with a ledge midway on which one could stand .
It have play an important part in the game of our uncle and aunt , be fortify castle , Indian ambush , throne , pulpit , or concert platform , a occasion require .
Uncle Edward have preach his first sermon at the age of eight from that old gray boulder ; and Aunt Julia , whose voice be to delight thousand , sing her early madrigal there .
The Story Girl mount to the ledge , sit on the rim , and look at u .
Pat sit gravely at it base and daintily wash his face with his black paw .
`` Now for your story about the orchard , '' say I . `` There be two important one , '' say the Story Girl .
`` The story of the Poet Who Was Kissed , and the Tale of the Family Ghost .
Which one shall I tell ? ''
`` Tell them both , '' say Felix greedily , `` but tell the ghost one first . ''
`` I do n't know . ''
The Story Girl look dubious .
`` That sort of story ought to be tell in the twilight among the shadow .
Then it would frighten the soul out of your body . ''
We think it might be more agreeable not to have the soul frighten out of our body , and we vote for the Family Ghost .
`` Ghost story be more comfortable in daytime , '' say Felix .
The Story Girl begin it and we listen avidly .
Cecily , who have hear it many time before , listen just as eagerly a we do .
She declare to me afterwards that no matter how often the Story Girl tell a story it always seem a new and exciting a if you have just hear it for the first time .
`` Long , long ago , '' begin the Story Girl , her voice give u an impression of remote antiquity , `` even before Grandfather King be bear , an orphan cousin of his live here with his parent .
Her name be Emily King .
She be very small and very sweet .
She have soft brown eye that be too timid to look straight at anybody -- like Cecily 's there -- and long , sleek , brown curl -- like mine ; and she have a tiny birthmark like a pink butterfly on one cheek -- right here .
`` Of course , there be no orchard here then .
It be just a field ; but there be a clump of white birch in it , right where that big , spread tree of Uncle Alec 's be now , and Emily like to sit among the fern under the birch and read or sew .
She have a lover .
His name be Malcolm Ward and he be as handsome a a prince .
She love him with all her heart and he love her the same ; but they have never speak about it .
They use to meet under the birch and talk about everything except love .
One day he tell her he be come the next day to ask A VERY IMPORTANT QUESTION , and he want to find her under the birch when he come .
Emily promise to meet him there .
I be sure she stay awake that night , think about it , and wonder what the important question would be , although she know perfectly well .
I would have .
